<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_husbandry">Birth of husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth of husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">Domestication of animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fat_tailed_sheep,_Afghanistan,_1976.jpg"><img alt="Fat-tailed sheep in Afghanistan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1106" data-file-width="1644" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/220px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/330px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/440px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fat_tailed_sheep,_Afghanistan,_1976.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The domestication of ruminants, like these <a href="/wiki/Fat-tailed_sheep" title="Fat-tailed sheep">fat-tailed sheep</a> in Afghanistan, provided <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_nomad" title="Pastoral nomad">nomads</a> across the Middle East and central Asia with a reliable source of food.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of livestock was driven by the need to have food on hand when hunting was unproductive. The desirable characteristics of a domestic animal are that it should be useful to man, should be able to thrive in his company, should breed freely and be easy to tend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clutton-Brock_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clutton-Brock-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domestication was not a single event, but a process repeated at various periods in different places. Sheep and goats were the animals that accompanied the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_nomad" title="Pastoral nomad">nomads</a> in the Middle East, while cattle and pigs were associated with more settled communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first wild animal to be domesticated was the <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dog</a>. Half-wild dogs, perhaps starting with young individuals, may have been tolerated as scavengers and killers of vermin, and being naturally <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">pack hunters</a>, were predisposed to become part of the human pack and join in the hunt. Prey animals, sheep, goats, pigs and cattle, were progressively domesticated early in the history of agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs were domesticated in Mesopotamia around 13,000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> followed, some time between 11,000 and 9,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated from the wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and Pakistan around 8,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>A cow was a great advantage to a villager as she produced more milk than her calf needed, and her strength could be put to use as a <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animal</a>, pulling a plough to increase production of crops, and drawing a sledge, and later a cart, to bring the produce home from the field. Draught animals were first used about 4,000 BC in the Middle East, increasing agricultural production immeasurably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-2">[2]</a></sup> In southern Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> was domesticated by 6,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fossilised chicken bones dated to 5040 BC have been found in northeastern China, far from where their wild ancestors lived in the jungles of tropical Asia, but archaeologists believe that the original purpose of domestication was for the sport of cockfighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, in South America, the <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a> had been domesticated, probably before 3,000 BC, as beasts of burden and for their wool. Neither was strong enough to pull a plough which limited the development of agriculture in the New World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses occur naturally on the steppes of Central Asia, and their domestication, around 3,000 BC in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> region, was originally as a source of meat; use as <a href="/wiki/Pack_animal" title="Pack animal">pack animals</a> and for riding followed. Around the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_wild_ass" title="Nubian wild ass">wild ass</a> was being tamed in Egypt. <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">Camels</a> were domesticated soon after this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285-8">[8]</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">Bactrian camel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_camel" title="Arabian camel">Arabian camel</a> becoming beasts of burden. By 1000 BC, caravans of Arabian camels were linking India with Mesopotamia and the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_civilisations">Ancient civilisations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient civilisations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg"><img alt="Egyptian hieroglyphic of cattle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3200" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/220px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/330px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/440px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milking cattle in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, cattle were the most important livestock, and sheep, goats, and pigs were also kept; poultry including ducks, geese, and pigeons were captured in nets and bred on farms, where they were force-fed with dough to fatten them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nile provided a plentiful source of fish. <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">Honey bees</a> were domesticated from at least the Old Kingdom, providing both honey and wax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, all the livestock known in ancient Egypt were available. In addition, rabbits were domesticated for food by the first century BC. To help flush them out from their underground burrows, the <a href="/wiki/Polecat" title="Polecat">polecat</a> was domesticated as the <a href="/wiki/Ferret" title="Ferret">ferret</a>, its use described by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_husbandry">Medieval husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Medieval husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg"><img alt="Painting of shepherd with sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="736" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/220px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/330px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/440px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shepherd with sheep in woven hurdle pen. Medieval France. 15th century, MS Douce 195</div></div></div></div><div>
</div><p>In northern Europe, agriculture including animal husbandry went into decline when the Roman empire collapsed. Some aspects such as the herding of animals continued throughout the period. By the 11th century, the economy had recovered and the countryside was again productive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> recorded every parcel of land and every animal in Britain: "there was not one single hide, nor a yard of land, nay, moreover ... not even an ox, nor a cow, nor a swine was there left, that was not set down in [the king's] writ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> For example, the royal <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earley" title="Earley">Earley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">Berkshire</a>, one of thousands of villages recorded in the book, had in 1086 "2 fisheries worth [paying tax of] 7s and 6d [each year] and 20 acres of meadow [for livestock]. Woodland for [feeding] 70 pigs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div>The improvements of animal husbandry in the medieval period in Europe went hand in hand with other developments. Improvements to the plough allowed the soil to be tilled to a greater depth. Horses took over from oxen as the main providers of traction, new ideas on crop rotation were developed and the growing of crops for winter fodder gained ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Peas, beans and vetches became common; they increased soil fertility through nitrogen fixation, allowing more livestock to be kept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-16">[16]</a></sup></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbian_Exchange">Columbian Exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Columbian Exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a></div>
<p>Exploration and colonisation of North and South America resulted in the introduction into Europe of such crops as maize, potatoes, sweet potatoes and manioc, while the principal Old World livestock – cattle, horses, sheep and goats – were introduced into the New World for the first time along with wheat, barley, rice and turnips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_Revolution">Agricultural Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Agricultural Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg"><img alt="Lincoln Longwool Sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/220px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/330px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/440px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_sheep" title="Lincoln sheep">Lincoln Longwool</a> breed was improved by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(agriculturalist)" title="Robert Bakewell (agriculturalist)">Robert Bakewell</a> in the 18th century.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">Selective breeding</a> for desired traits was established as a scientific practice by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(agriculturalist)" title="Robert Bakewell (agriculturalist)">Robert Bakewell</a> during the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a> in the 18th century. One of his most important breeding programs was with sheep. Using native stock, he was able to quickly select for large, yet fine-boned sheep, with long, lustrous wool. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_(sheep)" title="Lincoln (sheep)">Lincoln Longwool</a> was improved by Bakewell and in turn the Lincoln was used to develop the subsequent breed, named the New (or Dishley) Leicester. It was <a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">hornless</a> and had a square, meaty body with straight top lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> These sheep were exported widely and have contributed to numerous modern breeds. Under his influence, English farmers began to breed <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> for use primarily as beef. Long-horned heifers were crossed with the Westmoreland bull to create the <a href="/wiki/English_Longhorn" title="English Longhorn">Dishley Longhorn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The semi-natural, unfertilised pastures formed by traditional agricultural methods in Europe were managed by grazing and mowing. As the ecological impact of this land management strategy is similar to the impact of such natural disturbances as a <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfire</a>, this agricultural system shares many beneficial characteristics with a natural habitat, including the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. This strategy is declining in Europe today due to the intensification of agriculture. The mechanized and chemical methods used are causing biodiversity to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pykala2000_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pykala2000-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Husbandry">Husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">Livestock</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#Livestock_production_systems" title="Agriculture">Agriculture § Livestock production systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">Intensive animal farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg"><img alt="Herdwick sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/220px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/330px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/440px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herdwick_sheep" title="Herdwick sheep">Herdwick sheep</a> in an extensive <a href="/wiki/Hill_farming" title="Hill farming">hill farming</a> system, <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a>, England</div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, animal husbandry was part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" title="Subsistence farming">subsistence farmer's</a> way of life, producing not only the food needed by the family but also the fuel, fertiliser, clothing, transport and draught power. Killing the animal for food was a secondary consideration, and wherever possible its products, such as wool, eggs, milk and blood (by the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>) were harvested while the animal was still alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-21">[21]</a></sup>
In the traditional system of <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumance</a>, people and livestock moved seasonally between fixed summer and winter pastures; in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> regions the summer pasture was up in the mountains, the winter pasture in the valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals can be kept extensively or intensively. Extensive systems involve animals roaming at will, or under the supervision of a herdsman, often for their protection from <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">Ranching</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a> involves large herds of cattle grazing widely over public and private lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starrs_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starrs-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar cattle stations are found in South America, Australia and other places with large areas of land and low rainfall. Similar ranching systems have been used for <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levinson_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levinson-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the uplands of the United Kingdom, sheep are turned out on the fells in spring and graze the abundant mountain grasses untended, being brought to lower altitudes late in the year, with supplementary feeding being provided in winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In rural locations, <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> can obtain much of their nutrition from scavenging, and in African communities, hens may live for months without being fed, and still produce one or two eggs a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg"><img alt="Pigs in a barn" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1079" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/220px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/330px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/440px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pigs confined to a barn in an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">intensive system</a>, USA (midwest)</div></div></div>
<p>At the other extreme, in the more developed parts of the world, <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">animals are often intensively managed</a>; dairy cows may be kept in zero-grazing conditions with all their forage brought to them; beef cattle may be kept in high density <a href="/wiki/Feedlot" title="Feedlot">feedlots</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> pigs may be housed in climate-controlled buildings and never go outdoors;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> poultry may be reared in barns and kept in cages as laying birds under lighting-controlled conditions. In between these two extremes are semi-intensive, often family-run farms where livestock graze outside for much of the year, silage or hay is made to cover the times of year when the grass stops growing, and fertiliser, feed, and other inputs are brought onto the farm from outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blount_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blount-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">animal feed</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg"><img alt="Cattle around an outdoor feeder" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="640" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/220px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/330px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/440px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> around an outdoor feeder</div></div></div>
<p>Animals used as livestock are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a>, the main exception being the pig which is an omnivore. The herbivores can be divided into "concentrate selectors" which selectively feed on seeds, fruits and highly nutritious young foliage, "grazers" which mainly feed on grass, and "intermediate feeders" which choose their diet from the whole range of available plant material. Cattle, sheep, goats, deer and antelopes are <a href="/wiki/Ruminantia" title="Ruminantia">ruminants</a>; they digest food in two steps, chewing and swallowing in the normal way, and then regurgitating the semidigested cud to chew it again and thus extract the maximum possible food value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dryden_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dryden-29">[29]</a></sup>
The dietary needs of these animals is mostly met by eating grass. Grasses grow from the base of the leaf-blade, enabling it to thrive even when heavily grazed or cut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many climates grass growth is seasonal, for example in the temperate <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> or tropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainy_season" title="Rainy season">rainy season</a>, so some areas of the crop are set aside to be cut and preserved, either as <a href="/wiki/Hay" title="Hay">hay</a> (dried grass), or as <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> (fermented grass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Other forage crops are also grown and many of these, as well as crop residues, can be ensiled to fill the gap in the nutritional needs of livestock in the lean season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg"><img alt="Cattle feed pellets" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1000" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/220px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/330px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/440px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle feed pellets of pressed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linseed" title="Linseed">linseed</a></div></div></div>
<p>Extensively reared animals may subsist entirely on forage, but more intensively kept livestock will require energy and protein-rich foods in addition. Energy is mainly derived from cereals and cereal by-products, fats and oils and sugar-rich foods, while protein may come from fish or meat meal, milk products, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legumes" title="Legumes">legumes</a> and other plant foods, often the by-products of vegetable oil extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dryden2008_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dryden2008-33">[33]</a></sup>
Pigs and poultry are non-ruminants and unable to digest the <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in grass and other forages, so they are fed entirely on cereals and other high-energy foodstuffs. The ingredients for the animals' rations can be grown on the farm or can be bought, in the form of pelleted or cubed, compound foodstuffs specially formulated for the different classes of livestock, their growth stages and their specific nutritional requirements. Vitamins and minerals are added to balance the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSA_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FSA-34">[34]</a></sup> Farmed fish are usually fed pelleted food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSA_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FSA-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_breeding" title="Animal breeding">Animal breeding</a></div>
<p>The breeding of farm animals seldom occurs spontaneously but is managed by farmers with a view to encouraging certain traits that are seen as desirable. These include hardiness, prolificness, mothering abilities, fast growth rates, low feed consumption per unit of growth, better body proportions, higher yields, better fibre qualities and other characteristics. Undesirable traits such as health defects, aggressiveness or lack of docility are selected against.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selective breeding has been responsible for some large increases in productivity. In 2007, a typical broiler chicken at eight weeks old was 4.8 times as heavy as a bird of similar age in 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-35">[35]</a></sup> In the thirty years to 2007, the average milk yield of a dairy cow in the United States nearly doubled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inseminatie_(4494727256).jpg"><img alt="artificial insemination" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3215" data-file-width="4823" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg/220px-Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg/330px-Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg/440px-Inseminatie_%284494727256%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inseminatie_(4494727256).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">Artificial insemination</a></div></div></div>
<p>Techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">artificial insemination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embryo_transfer" title="Embryo transfer">embryo transfer</a> are frequently used today, not only as methods to guarantee that females breed regularly but also to help improve herd genetics. This may be done by transplanting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryos" title="Embryos">embryos</a> from high-quality females into lower-quality surrogate mothers – freeing up the higher-quality mother to be reimpregnated. This practice vastly increases the number of offspring which may be produced by a small selection of the best quality parent animals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On one hand, this improves the ability of the animals to convert feed to meat, milk, or fiber more efficiently, and improve the quality of the final product. On the other, it decreases <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>, increasing the severity of certain disease outbreaks among other risks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_health">Animal health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Animal health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">Veterinary medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg"><img alt="Vaccination of a goat" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/220px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/330px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/440px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccinating</a> a <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a></div></div></div>
<p>Good husbandry, proper feeding, and <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> are the main contributors to animal health on the farm, bringing economic benefits through maximised production. When, despite these precautions, animals still become sick, they are treated with <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">veterinary medicines</a>, by the farmer and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a>. In the European Union, when farmers treat their own animals, they are required to follow the guidelines for treatment and to record the treatments given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
Animals are susceptible to a number of diseases and conditions that may affect their health. Some, like <a href="/wiki/Classical_swine_fever" title="Classical swine fever">classical swine fever</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scrapie" title="Scrapie">scrapie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> are specific to one type of stock, while others, like <a href="/wiki/Foot-and-mouth_disease" title="Foot-and-mouth disease">foot-and-mouth disease</a> affect all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloven-hoofed" title="Cloven-hoofed">cloven-hoofed</a> animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Where the condition is serious, governments impose regulations on import and export, on the movement of stock, <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> restrictions and the reporting of suspected cases. <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">Vaccines</a> are available against certain diseases, and antibiotics are widely used where appropriate. At one time, antibiotics were routinely added to certain compound foodstuffs to promote growth, but this practice is now frowned on in many countries because of the risk that it may lead to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals living under intensive conditions are particularly prone to internal and external parasites; increasing numbers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_lice" title="Sea lice">sea lice</a> are affecting farmed salmon in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Reducing the parasite burdens of livestock results in increased productivity and profitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Governments are particularly concerned with <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonoses</a>, diseases that humans may acquire from animals. Wild animal populations may harbour diseases that can affect domestic animals which may acquire them as a result of insufficient <a href="/wiki/Biosecurity" title="Biosecurity">biosecurity</a>. An outbreak of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nipah_virus" title="Nipah virus">Nipah virus</a> in Malaysia in 1999 was traced back to pigs becoming ill after contact with fruit-eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_fox" title="Flying fox">flying foxes</a>, their faeces and urine. The pigs in turn passed the infection to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chua_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-chua-44">[44]</a></sup> Avian flu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N1" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> is present in wild bird populations and can be carried large distances by migrating birds. This virus is easily transmissible to domestic poultry, and to humans living in close proximity with them. Other infectious diseases affecting wild animals, farm animals and humans include <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leptospirosis" title="Leptospirosis">leptospirosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brucellosis" title="Brucellosis">brucellosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_bovis" title="Mycobacterium bovis">tuberculosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trichinosis" title="Trichinosis">trichinosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norrgren_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norrgren-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farmyard_Drawing.jpg"><img alt="Watercolor drawing of farmyard with cow, horse, pigs, and chickens" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="466" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/220px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/330px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/440px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farmyard_Drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Familiar livestock: ink and watercolour drawing of a farmyard with cow, horse, pigs, and chickens, 1869</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_of_species">Range of species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Range of species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Livestock#Types" title="Livestock">Livestock § Types</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture#Species_groups" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture § Species groups</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a></div>
<p>There is no single universally agreed definition of which species are livestock. Widely agreed types of livestock include cattle for beef and dairy, sheep, goats, pigs, and poultry. Various other species are sometimes considered livestock, such as horses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> while poultry birds are sometimes excluded. In some parts of the world, livestock includes species such as buffalo, and the South American camelids, the alpaca and llama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Some authorities use much broader definitions to include fish in aquaculture, micro-livestock such as rabbits and guinea pigs, as well as insects from honey bees to crickets raised for human consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Merino_shearing.jpg"><img alt="Sheering merino sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="350" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg/220px-Merino_shearing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg/330px-Merino_shearing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Merino_shearing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shearing" title="Shearing">Shearing</a> a <a href="/wiki/Merino" title="Merino">Merino</a> sheep for its <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_product" title="Animal product">Animal product</a></div>
<p>Animals are raised for a wide variety of products, principally <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>, but also including <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isinglass" title="Isinglass">isinglass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rennet" title="Rennet">rennet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Animals are also kept for more specialised purposes, such as to produce <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antiserum" title="Antiserum">antiserum</a> (containing <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>) for medical use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Where fodder or other crops are grown alongside animals, <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> can serve as a fertiliser, returning minerals and organic matter to the soil in a semi-closed organic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches">Branches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Branches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dairy">Dairy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dairy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">Dairy farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_(5).jpg"><img alt="Rotary milking parlour" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/220px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/330px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/440px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_(5).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern rotary milking parlour, Germany</div></div></div>
<p>Although all mammals produce milk to nourish their young, the cow is predominantly used throughout the world to produce milk and milk products for human consumption. Other animals used to a lesser extent for this purpose include sheep, goats, camels, buffaloes, yaks, reindeer, horses and donkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdairy_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdairy-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>All these animals have been domesticated over the centuries, being bred for such desirable characteristics as fecundity, productivity, docility and the ability to thrive under the  prevailing conditions. Whereas in the past, cattle had multiple functions, modern dairy cow breeding has resulted in specialised <a href="/wiki/Holstein_Friesian_cattle" title="Holstein Friesian cattle">Holstein Friesian-type</a> animals that produce large quantities of milk economically. <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">Artificial insemination</a> is widely available to allow farmers to select for the particular traits that suit their circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAObreeding_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAObreeding-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas in the past, cows were kept in small herds on family farms, grazing pastures and being fed hay in winter, nowadays there is a trend towards larger herds, more intensive systems, the feeding of <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> and "zero grazing", a system where grass is cut and brought to the cow, which is housed year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many communities, milk production is only part of the purpose of keeping an animal which may also be used as a beast of burden or to draw a plough, or for the production of fibre, meat and leather, with the <a href="/wiki/Cow_dung" title="Cow dung">dung</a> being used for fuel or for the improvement of <a href="/wiki/Soil_fertility" title="Soil fertility">soil fertility</a>. Sheep and goats may be favoured for dairy production in climates and conditions that do not suit dairy cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdairy_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdairy-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat">Meat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Meat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg"><img alt="Hereford cow" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="550" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/220px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/330px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/440px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hereford_(cattle)" title="Hereford (cattle)">Hereford</a> is a hardy breed of <a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a>, now raised in many countries around the world.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">Meat industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep_farming" title="Sheep farming">Sheep farming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pig_farming" title="Pig farming">Pig farming</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">Meat</a>, mainly from farmed animals, is a major source of dietary <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> around the world, averaging about 8% of man's energy intake. The actual types eaten depend on local preferences, availability, cost and other factors, with cattle, sheep, pigs and goats being the main species involved. Cattle generally produce a single offspring annually which takes more than a year to mature; sheep and goats often have twins and these are ready for slaughter in less than a year; pigs are more prolific, producing more than one litter of up to about 11<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> piglets each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory-60">[60]</a></sup> Horses, donkeys, deer, buffalo, llamas, alpacas, guanacos and vicunas are farmed for meat in various regions. Some desirable traits of animals raised for meat include fecundity, hardiness, fast growth rate, ease of management and high food conversion efficiency. About half of the world's meat is produced from animals grazing on open ranges or on enclosed pastures, the other half being produced intensively in various factory-farming systems; these are mostly cows, pigs or poultry, and often reared indoors, typically at high densities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poultry">Poultry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Poultry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battery_hens_-Bastos,_Sao_Paulo,_Brazil-31March2007.jpg"><img alt="Battery hens" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="321" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg/170px-Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg/255px-Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battery_hens_-Bastos,_Sao_Paulo,_Brazil-31March2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battery hens, Brazil</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">Poultry farming</a></div>
<p>Poultry, kept for their eggs and for their meat, include chickens, turkeys, geese and ducks. The great majority of laying birds used for egg production are chickens. Methods for keeping layers range from free-range systems, where the birds can roam as they will but are housed at night for their own protection, through semi-intensive systems where they are housed in barns and have perches, litter and some freedom of movement, to intensive systems where they are kept in cages. The <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cages</a> are arranged in long rows in multiple tiers, with external feeders, drinkers, and egg collection facilities. This is the most labour saving and economical method of egg production but has been criticised on animal welfare grounds as the birds are unable to exhibit their normal behaviours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the developed world, the majority of the poultry reared for meat is raised indoors in big sheds, with automated equipment under environmentally controlled conditions. Chickens raised in this way are known as broilers, and genetic improvements have meant that they can be grown to slaughter weight within six or seven weeks of hatching. Newly hatched chicks are restricted to a small area and given supplementary heating. Litter on the floor absorbs the droppings and the area occupied is expanded as they grow. Feed and water is supplied automatically and the lighting is controlled. The birds may be harvested on several occasions or the whole shed may be cleared at one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>A similar rearing system is usually used for turkeys, which are less hardy than chickens, but they take longer to grow and are often moved on to separate fattening units to finish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Ducks are particularly popular in Asia and Australia and can be killed at seven weeks under commercial conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Aquaculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg"><img alt="Freshwater fish farm" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3064" data-file-width="4597" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/220px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/330px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/440px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Freshwater <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">fish farming</a>, France</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a></div>
<p>Aquaculture has been defined as "the farming of aquatic organisms including fish, molluscs, crustaceans and aquatic plants and implies some form of intervention in the rearing process to enhance production, such as regular stocking, feeding, protection from predators, etc. Farming also implies individual or <a href="/wiki/Corporate_farming" title="Corporate farming">corporate ownership</a> of the stock being cultivated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In practice it can take place in the sea or in freshwater, and be extensive or intensive. Whole bays, lakes or ponds may be devoted to aquaculture, or the farmed animal may be retained in cages (fish), artificial reefs, racks or strings (shellfish). Fish and prawns can be cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">rice paddies</a>, either arriving naturally or being introduced, and both crops can be harvested together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fish_hatchery" title="Fish hatchery">Fish hatcheries</a> provide larval and juvenile fish, crustaceans and shellfish, for use in aquaculture systems. When large enough these are transferred to growing-on tanks and sold to fish farms to reach harvest size. Some species that are commonly raised in hatcheries include <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prawn" title="Prawn">prawns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilapia" title="Tilapia">tilapia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scallops</a>. Similar facilities can be used to raise species with conservation needs to be released into the wild, or game fish for restocking waterways. Important aspects of husbandry at these early stages include selection of breeding stock, control of water quality and nutrition. In the wild, there is a massive amount of mortality at the nursery stage; farmers seek to minimise this while at the same time maximising growth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosig_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosig-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Insects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG"><img alt="Crickets" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/220px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/330px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/440px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">Crickets</a> being raised <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">for human consumption</a>, Thailand</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Beekeeping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">Bees</a> have been kept in hives since at least the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty of Egypt</a>, five thousand years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and man had been harvesting honey from the wild long before that. Fixed comb hives are used in many parts of the world and are made from any locally available material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In more advanced economies, where modern strains of domestic bee have been selected for docility and productiveness, various designs of hive are used which enable the combs to be removed for processing and extraction of honey. Quite apart from the <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a> they produce, honey bees are important <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a> of crops and wild plants, and in many places hives are transported around the countryside to assist in pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulture</a>, the rearing of silkworms, was first adopted by the Chinese during the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The only species farmed commercially is the domesticated <a href="/wiki/Bombyx_mori" title="Bombyx mori">silkmoth</a>. When it spins its <a href="/wiki/Pupa#Cocoon" title="Pupa">cocoon</a>, each larva produces an exceedingly long, slender thread of silk. The larvae feed on <a href="/wiki/Morus_alba" title="Morus alba">mulberry leaves</a> and in Europe, only one generation is normally raised each year as this is a deciduous tree. In China, Korea and Japan however, two generations are normal, and in the tropics, multiple generations are expected. Most production of silk occurs in the Far East, with a synthetic diet being used to rear the silkworms in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insects form part of the human diet in many cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carrington2010_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carrington2010-74">[74]</a></sup> In Thailand, <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">crickets</a> are farmed for this purpose in the north of the country, and <a href="/wiki/Rhynchophorus_ferrugineus" title="Rhynchophorus ferrugineus">palm weevil</a> larvae in the south. The crickets are kept in pens, boxes or drawers and fed on commercial pelleted poultry food, while the palm weevil larvae live on <a href="/wiki/Cabbage_palm" title="Cabbage palm">cabbage palm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sago_palm" title="Sago palm">sago palm</a> trees, which limits their production to areas where these trees grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangkok_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bangkok-75">[75]</a></sup> Another delicacy of this region is the <a href="/wiki/Omphisa_fuscidentalis" title="Omphisa fuscidentalis">bamboo caterpillar</a>, and the best rearing and harvesting techniques in semi-natural habitats are being studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangkok_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bangkok-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact<span id="Environmental_Impact"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_livestock" title="Environmental impact of livestock">Environmental impact of livestock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">Environmental impact of meat production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg"><img alt="Cattle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/220px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/330px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/440px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Livestock production requires large areas of land.</div></div></div>
<p>Animal husbandry has a significant impact on the world environment. It is responsible for somewhere between 20 and 33% of the fresh water usage in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and livestock, and the production of feed for them, occupy about a third of the earth's ice-free land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Livestock production is a contributing factor in species <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Animal agriculture contributes to species extinction in various ways. Habitat is destroyed by clearing forests and converting land to grow feed crops and for animal grazing, while predators and herbivores are frequently targeted and hunted because of a perceived threat to livestock profits; for example, animal husbandry is responsible for up to 91% of the <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">deforestation in the Amazon region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In addition, livestock produce <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>. Cows produce some 570 million cubic metres of methane per day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> that accounts for from 35 to 40% of the overall methane emissions of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOshadow_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOshadow-82">[82]</a></sup> Livestock is responsible for 65% of all human-related emissions of the powerful and long-lived greenhouse gas <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOshadow_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOshadow-82">[82]</a></sup> As a result, ways of mitigating animal husbandry's environmental impact are being studied. Strategies include using <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> from manure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare">Animal welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Animal welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">Animal welfare</a></div>
<p>Since the 18th century, people have become increasingly concerned about <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">the welfare of farm animals</a>. Possible measures of welfare include <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, freedom from <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and freedom from <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppression</a>. Standards and laws for animal welfare have been created worldwide, broadly in line with the most widely held position in the western world, a form of <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>: that it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_philosophy" title="Moral philosophy">morally acceptable</a> for humans to use non-human animals, provided that no unnecessary suffering is caused, and that the benefits to humans outweigh the costs to the livestock. An opposing view is that <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animals have rights</a>, should not be regarded as property, and should never be used by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Live_export" title="Live export">Live export</a> of animals has risen to meet increased global demand for livestock such as in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. Animal rights activists have objected to long-distance transport of animals; one result was the banning of live exports from New Zealand in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_budget;_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg"><img alt="Cartoon of John Bull giving his breeches to save his bacon" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1785" data-file-width="2400" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/220px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/330px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/440px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_budget;_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Opening of the budget; – or – <a href="/wiki/John_Bull" title="John Bull">John Bull</a> giving his <a href="/wiki/Breeches" title="Breeches">breeches</a> to <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/save_someone%27s_bacon" title="wikt:save someone's bacon">save his bacon</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[a]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a> (d. 1815)</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 18th century, the farmer <a href="/wiki/John_Bull" title="John Bull">John Bull</a> has represented <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">English national identity</a>, first in <a href="/wiki/John_Arbuthnot" title="John Arbuthnot">John Arbuthnot</a>'s political satires, and soon afterwards in cartoons by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a> and others including <a href="/wiki/John_Tenniel" title="John Tenniel">John Tenniel</a>. He likes food, beer, dogs, horses, and <a href="/wiki/Field_sports" title="Field sports">country sports</a>; he is practical and down to earth, and anti-intellectual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg"><img alt="Illustration of dressed pigs" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="933" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/170px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/255px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/340px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dressed pigs in <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s 1913 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Pigling_Bland" title="The Tale of Pigling Bland">The Tale of Pigling Bland</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Farm animals are widespread in books and songs for children; the reality of animal husbandry is often distorted, softened, or idealized, giving children an almost entirely fictitious account of farm life. The books often depict a rural idyll of happy animals free to roam in attractive countryside, which is completely at odds with the realities of the impersonal, mechanized activities involved in modern intensive farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoult_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoult-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs, for example, appear in several of <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s "little books", as Piglet in <a href="/wiki/A._A._Milne" title="A. A. Milne">A. A. Milne</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winnie_the_Pooh" title="Winnie the Pooh">Winnie the Pooh</a> stories, and somewhat more darkly (with a hint of animals going to slaughter) as Babe in <a href="/wiki/Dick_King-Smith" title="Dick King-Smith">Dick King-Smith</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sheep-Pig" title="The Sheep-Pig">The Sheep-Pig</a></i>, and as Wilbur in <i><a href="/wiki/Charlotte%27s_Web" title="Charlotte's Web">Charlotte's Web</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Pigs tend to be "bearers of cheerfulness, good humour and innocence". Many of these books are completely <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a>, dressing farm animals in clothes and having them walk on two legs, live in houses, and perform human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoult_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoult-92">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_music" title="Children's music">children's song</a> "<a href="/wiki/Old_MacDonald_Had_a_Farm" title="Old MacDonald Had a Farm">Old MacDonald Had a Farm</a>" describes a farmer named MacDonald and the various animals he keeps, celebrating the noises they each make.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many urban children experience animal husbandry for the first time at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petting_farm" title="Petting farm">petting farm</a>; in Britain, some five million people a year visit a farm of some kind. This presents some risk of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis#Farming.2C_ranching_and_animal_husbandry" title="Zoonosis">infection</a>, especially if children handle animals and then fail to <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">wash their hands</a>; a strain of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli" title="E. coli">E. coli</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infected</a> 93 people who had visited an interactive farm in an outbreak in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Historic farms such as those in the United States offer farmstays and "a carefully curated version of farming to those willing to pay for it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2014_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2014-96">[95]</a></sup> sometimes giving visitors a romanticised image of a <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a> ideal from an unspecified time in the pre-industrial past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2014_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2014-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
